        function gptClean(gptText) {
            if (typeof (gptText) == "string") {
                gptText = gptText.replace(/\&/g, '%26');
                return gptText.replace(/\^|\*|#|\$|\"|\'|\=|\,|\!|\+|\~|\;|\(|\)|\<|\>|\[|\]|\?|\%|\:|\@|\;|\&|\||\{|\}|\\|/g, '');
            }
            return "blank";
        }

            .setTargeting('property', 'value-of-property')    // Change and duplicate this line for as many additional keywords to send
            .setTargeting('doi', gptClean('10.1002/aenm.202400371')) // Publication DOI
            .setTargeting('pbPage', gptClean(gptPage))
            .setTargeting('pbSubPage', gptClean(gptSubPage))
            .setTargeting('urlpath', gptClean(document.location.pathname));

            .defineSizeMapping(sizes.leaderboard)

    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="792b83ab9e3549f0b7ebcf1ade6e36bb">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: 'f4817d4f-f544-4c30-97be-f6c88037f6ba'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

   <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d190217489" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>Ferrous sulfides with the high theoretic capacity are the promising anode for sodium ion batteries. However, capacity fading and inferior rate capability still hinder their practical application. In this work, Na-doped Fe<sub>7</sub>S<sub>8</sub> microrods with cationic vacancies and weakened Fe─S bond are constructed through a facile and scalable sulfurized route. The experimental results combined with theoretical analysis thoroughly reveal the generation of Fe vacancies and weakened Fe─S bond strength induced by sodium doping, which modulates the energy band structure of Na-doped Fe<sub>7</sub>S<sub>8</sub>, provides more active sites, and accelerates the sodiation/desodiation reaction kinetics, simultaneously. Moreover, the pre-doping sodium delivers a strong guiding effect on the formation of thin and stable solid electrolyte interface films. As the result, the optimal sample exhibits the excellent sodium storage performance, including the high and stable reversible capacity (674 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> after 200 cycles at 0.5 A g<sup>−1</sup> and 503 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> after 1500 cycles at 10 A g<sup>−1</sup>), superior rate capability, and increased initial coulombic efficiency. Furthermore, the full cell paired with commercial Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> also displays the outstanding cyclic stability with 95.9% capacity retention at 0.5 A g<sup>−1</sup> after 100 cycles.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="aenm202400371-sec-0010">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="aenm202400371-sec-0010-title">1 Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>With the wide application of lithium-ion batteries (LIBs) in the various industries, the demand for lithium resources is increased rapidly, which is exacerbated by the lithium resource scarcity and uneven distribution. Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs), with the advantages of low cost, abundant reserves and similar mechanism to LIBs, present a promising alternative to replace the LIBs in the fields of energy storage station and electric vehicles.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400371-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400371-bib-0001_R_d190217481e504" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Nat. Commun.">1</a>-<a href="#aenm202400371-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400371-bib-0007_R_d190217481e504" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Adv. Energy Mater.">7</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In view of the incapacity of commercial graphite to accommodate the Na<sup>+</sup>, the search for reliable anode materials has emerged as a focal point in the field of SIBs. Transition metal sulfides (TMSs) are considering as the potential anode for SIBs, due to their open framework structure, high theoretical specific capacity, low cost and multiple sodium storage mechanisms. The relatively weaker metal─sulfur bonds compared with metal oxides is a critical factor for the theoretically higher electrochemical activity. However, the application of these TMSs is always hindered by afflictive capacity fading and inferiorrate capability, causing by the combined effects of volumetric expansion, poor electric-conductivity and sodium polysulfides dissolution &amp; diffusion.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400371-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400371-bib-0008_R_d190217481e511" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Adv. Energy Mater.">8</a>-<a href="#aenm202400371-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400371-bib-0012_R_d190217481e511" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">12</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>Among the various TMSs, iron sulfides are widely regarded as the highly promising anode material for SIBs, due to their abundant resources, diverse phase structures, electrocatalysis of iron element and easy synthesis process.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400371-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400371-bib-0013_R_d190217481e518" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">13</a>, <a href="#aenm202400371-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400371-bib-0014_R_d190217481e518" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - J. Mater. Chem. A">14</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Unfortunately, they are also exhibit the common defects found in TMSs.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400371-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400371-bib-0015_R_d190217481e523" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - J. Mater. Chem. A">15</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Rational structural construction (carbon coating and nanostructure) would be seemed as an effective route to improve the electrochemical performances of iron sulfides-based anodes. For example, SnS<sub>2</sub>/FeS<sub>2</sub> heterostructures covalently attached to reduced graphene oxide (rGO) endows a reversible capacity of 653.3 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> after 50 cycles at 0.2 A g<sup>−1</sup>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400371-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400371-bib-0016_R_d190217481e537" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Chem. Eng. J.">16</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Hollow bimetallic CoFeS@rGO shows a high reversible capacity (661.9 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.1 A g<sup>−1</sup>), long cycle life (84.8% capacity retention after 1500 cycles at 1 A g<sup>−1</sup>).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400371-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400371-bib-0017_R_d190217481e549" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Adv. Energy Mater.">17</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In addition, heteroatom doping is a simple and feasible method to enhance the electrochemical performance of single metal sulfides, which is more reliable and scalable than the heterojunction construction. Different valence state doping elements would endow the host unexpected improvements on the electrochemical performances and regulate the electronic band structure, causing by the induced structural vacancies, such as sulfur vacancies,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400371-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400371-bib-0018_R_d190217481e554" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Inorg. Chem. Front.">18</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Te vacancies,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400371-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400371-bib-0019_R_d190217481e560" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Adv. Energy Mater.">19</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> iron vacancies,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400371-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400371-bib-0020_R_d190217481e565" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Adv. Sci.">20</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> etc. Besides, the introduction of alien atoms with larger ionic radius enhances the lattice parameters to accommodate more sodium ions.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400371-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400371-bib-0021_R_d190217481e570" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - Chem. Eng. J.">21</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In previous studies, various heteroatom doping methods have been applied to enhance the performance of FeS<sub>x</sub>, including nitrogen doping,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400371-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400371-bib-0022_R_d190217481e578" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - Electrochim. Acta">22</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> sulfur doping,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400371-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400371-bib-0023_R_d190217481e583" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - Appl. Surf. Sci.">23</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> cobalt doping.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400371-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400371-bib-0024_R_d190217481e589" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - Adv. Energy Mater.">24</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> For instance, the N-doped Fe<sub>7</sub>S<sub>8</sub> nanoparticles embedding in an N, S co-doped porous carbon framework exhibits a high reversible capacity of 489 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 5 A g<sup>−1</sup>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400371-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400371-bib-0025_R_d190217481e603" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - Molecules">25</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The Co-doped iron sulfide polyhedrons delivers a high reversible capacity of 700 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.1 A g<sup>−1</sup> and a high discharge capacity of 164 mAh g<sup>-1</sup> after 1000 cycles at 1 A g<sup>–1</sup>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400371-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400371-bib-0024_R_d190217481e617" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - Adv. Energy Mater.">24</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Notably, the bond strength of M─Se is weaker than that of M─S bonds. The substituting of sulfur with selenium would lead to an enhanced electrochemical performance. Yang's group present a solid-solution of S/Se in FeS<sub>2-</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>Se<i><sub>x</sub></i> with the exceptional cyclic and rate performances, which attributed to the synergistic effect of high capacity of FeS<sub>2</sub> and favorable reaction kinetics of FeSe<sub>2</sub>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400371-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400371-bib-0026_R_d190217481e634" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">26</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The proposed approach essentially weakens the strength of M─S bonds to improve the reaction kinetics. Nevertheless, considering the price and toxicity of selenium sources, it is significant to explore a novel and facile method to weaken the M─S bonds for the superior SIBs anode.</p>
         
         <p>Herein, a sodium-doped ferrous sulfide is synthesized through a solid reaction between MIL-88A(Fe) and Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub> under an argon atmosphere (<b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400371-fig-0001">1a</a>). The metal-organic framework (MOF) precursor not only ensures a porous 1D microrod structure with carbon coating, but also alleviates the agglomeration of nano active materials by the well-dispersed metal sites. The partial occupation of Fe sties by doped Na atoms induces the generation of iron vacancies, thereby weakening the strength of Fe─S bond, and accelerating the kinetics of sodiation/desodiation reactions. Moreover, the pre-doping sodium would guide the deposition of Na-containing components to form the thin and stable solid electrolyte interface (SEI) films. When served as the anode material for SIBs, the optimized sample exhibits the superior cycling performance (maintaining 674 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> after 200 cycles at 0.5 A g<sup>−1</sup> and 503 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> after 1500 cycles at 10 A g<sup>−1</sup>) and improved rate capability. In addition, the full cell paired with commercial Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> also delivers a high reversible capacity of 498 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.1 A g<sup>−1</sup> (calculated based on the weight of the anode material), and a capacity retention rate of about 95.9% after 100 cycles at 0.5 A g<sup>−1</sup>.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="aenm202400371-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/19f3960d-815b-4ae3-b4bf-4a7c54c73628/aenm202400371-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>

         </section>
      </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="aenm202400371-sec-0020">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="aenm202400371-sec-0020-title">2 Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <p>The impact of Na-doping on the Fe<sub>7</sub>S<sub>8</sub> is theoretically analyzed using density functional theory (DFT) calculations. First, the electron localization function (ELF) is carried out to analyses the electronic effect of Na-doping for Fe<sub>7</sub>S<sub>8</sub>. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400371-fig-0001">1b</a>, the similar spin electron pair density between Fe─S is observed in the <i>ab</i> plane. However, the ELF values of Na-doped Fe<sub>7</sub>S<sub>8</sub> (denoted as NFS-1) around Fe atom are lower than those of bare Fe<sub>7</sub>S<sub>8</sub> (denoted as FS), indicating a decreased strength of Fe─S bond affected by Na-doping. DFT calculation results indicate that NFS-1 deliver a higher affinity for Na<sup>+</sup> adsorption (−0.66&nbsp;eV) compared to the bare FS (−0.56&nbsp;eV), which would be attributed to the capability of Fe vacancies to capture Na<sup>+</sup> during the discharge states (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>, Supporting Information).Moreover, the projected density of states (DOS) of FS, NFS and double Na-doped Fe<sub>7</sub>S<sub>8</sub> (denoted as NFS-2) are depicted in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400371-fig-0001">1c–e</a>. The electronic structure of FS exhibits the typical metallic features with the continuous electron distribution, which establishes its possibility as advanced SIBs anode material. Both NFS-1 and NFS-2 show an increased energy state near Fermi level (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a>, Supporting Information), meaning the potential of accelerated electron/ion transfer by Na-doping. According to the Na-ion diffusion barriers illustrated in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400371-fig-0001">1f</a>, the lowest Na-ion diffusion energy barriers endow NFS-1 the feasibility for excellent rate capability.</p>
         
         <p>The phase and crystal structure of as-prepared samples are first determined by XRD. As shown in <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400371-fig-0002">2a</a>, all the diffraction peaks of FS are corresponded well to the hexagonal Fe<sub>7</sub>S<sub>8</sub> (JCPDS: 71-0591) without any impure peaks. After the addition of Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub>, the XRD patterns of as-prepared NFS-1 and NFS-2 exhibit the same profile as the bare Fe<sub>7</sub>S<sub>8</sub>. Notably, the most prominent diffraction (206) peak for both NFS-1 and NFS-2 has shifted to the left, indicating the enlarged lattice patterns affected by the sodium doping.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400371-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400371-bib-0015_R_d190217481e775" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - J. Mater. Chem. A">15</a>, <a href="#aenm202400371-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400371-bib-0021_R_d190217481e775" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - Chem. Eng. J.">21</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The content of Na in NFS-1 is approximately 10% of the Fe atom, as determined by ICP-OES result. EPR is a physical method to detect lone paired electrons in materials, meaning the presence of structural defects or vacancies.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400371-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400371-bib-0027_R_d190217481e781" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">27</a>, <a href="#aenm202400371-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400371-bib-0028_R_d190217481e781" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">28</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400371-fig-0002">2b</a>, both Na-doped samples exhibit obvious symmetrical peak, indicating the presence of lone paired electrons. It provides strong evidence of the structural defects in the Na-doped samples.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="aenm202400371-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/63099826-51c1-4dc4-9a95-25ffe397ee77/aenm202400371-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/63099826-51c1-4dc4-9a95-25ffe397ee77/aenm202400371-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/0a655463-513c-49d2-9e1a-8583c80a4083/aenm202400371-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/63099826-51c1-4dc4-9a95-25ffe397ee77/aenm202400371-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=aenm202400371-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Faenm.202400371" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Structural characterizations of as-prepared samples: a) XRD patterns; b) EPR spectra; high-resolution XPS spectra of c) Fe 2p and d) S 2p; e) Fe K-edge XANES spectra of FS, NFS-1, NFS-2, Fe foil, Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>, FeO and FeS; f) the EXAFS spectra of FS, NFS-1 and NFS-2 with the standard references Fe foil and FeS; g) WT-EXAFS signals of FS, NFS-1 and NFS-2.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>XPS is carried out to analyze the surface elemental composition and chemical state of as-prepared samples. In Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400371-fig-0002">2c</a>, the high-resolution Fe 2p spectra exhibit the decrease in peak intensity at 707.1&nbsp;eV (the characteristic peak of Fe─S bond), indicating the reduction strength of Fe─S bond.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400371-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400371-bib-0029_R_d190217481e819" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - Energy Environ. Mater.">29</a>, <a href="#aenm202400371-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400371-bib-0030_R_d190217481e819" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - Appl. Phys. A">30</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> This conjecture is verified in the S 2p spectra (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400371-fig-0002">2d</a>). The fitted peak at 161.3&nbsp;eV corresponding to the M─S bond also presents decreasing intensity by the Na-doping. Moreover, the presence of C─S bond (163.7&nbsp;eV) is a strong evidence for the S-doping in carbon layer to increase their active sites and electroconductivity.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400371-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400371-bib-0031_R_d190217481e827" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - Chem. Eng. J.">31</a>, <a href="#aenm202400371-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400371-bib-0032_R_d190217481e827" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - ACS Nano">32</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The C 1s spectra of NFS-1 are divided as three peaks, corresponding to the C─C/C═C (284.8&nbsp;eV), C─S, and O─C═O bonds,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400371-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400371-bib-0033_R_d190217481e833" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33 - J. Phys. Chem. C">33</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> respectively (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a>, Supporting Information). Besides, the obvious Na 1s peaks demonstrate the successfully Na-doping into the Fe<sub>7</sub>S<sub>8</sub> lattice (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a>, Supporting Information). The chemical state and bond strength of as-prepared samples are further determined by XAFS. As depicted in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400371-fig-0002">2e</a> and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5</a> (Supporting Information), the spectra of Fe K-edge X-ray absorption near-edge structure (XANES) reveal that the valence states of Fe in NFS-1 and NFS-2 are between bivalent and trivalent, and gradually decrease with the incorporation of sodium ions.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400371-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400371-bib-0034_R_d190217481e855" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 34 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">34</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The valence state of Fe in NFS-1 (2.367) is higher than that of NFS-2 (2.330), as determined by LCF fitting method (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6</a>, Supporting Information). Furthermore, the detail coordination structure of Fe in the as-prepared samples are disclosed by Fourier-transformed extended X-ray absorption fine structure (FT-EXAFS) and depicted in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400371-fig-0002">2f</a>. The FT-EXAFS fitting curves and detail structural parameters by the multiple shells fitting of <i>R</i> space are shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7</a> and Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a> (Supporting Information). Notably, the Fe─S bond length of NFS-1 and NFS-2 increases from 2.181 Å to 2.355 and 2.316 Å (after phase position correction), respectively, indicating a discernible weakening in the strength of Fe─S bond.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400371-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400371-bib-0035_R_d190217481e875" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 35 - Small">35</a>, <a href="#aenm202400371-bib-0036" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400371-bib-0036_R_d190217481e875" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 36 - J. Mater. Chem.">36</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The second shell of NFS-1 delivers a sight right shift compared to that of FS and standard FeS, which would be attributed to the formation of Fe─S─Na path (3.242 Å) by the Na-doping. Additionally, the Fe atom coordination number (<i>N</i>) of Fe─S─Na path in NFS-1 is decreased from 3 (Fe─S─Fe path in FS) to 1, which provides a powerful support of the presence of Fe vacancies. The wavelet transforms of EXAFS (WT-EXAFS) spectra in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400371-fig-0002">2g</a> further confirm the scattering path of Fe─S/Fe─S─Fe, Fe─S/Fe─S─Fe(Na) and Fe─S/Fe─S─Fe(Na) paths in FS, NFS-1 and NFS-2, respectively.</p>
         
         <p>In Raman spectra (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8</a>, Supporting Information), all the as-prepared samples exhibit two characteristic peaks of carbon, which are assigned to the defect carbon (D-band, 1310 cm<sup>−1</sup>) and graphite carbon (G-band, 1580 cm<sup>−1</sup>), respectively. The <i>I</i><sub>D</sub>/<i>I</i><sub>G</sub> value of FS, NFS-1, and NFS-2 is 0.9, 1.03, and 1.07, respectively, demonstrating that sodium doping would enhance the proportion of disorder carbon in the pyrolytic carbon layer, thereby endowing NFS-1 and NFS-2 with more active sites for facilitating sodium storage.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400371-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400371-bib-0018_R_d190217481e903" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Inorg. Chem. Front.">18</a>, <a href="#aenm202400371-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400371-bib-0037_R_d190217481e903" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 37 - CrystEngComm">37</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Furthermore, the content of carbon in NFS-1 is analyzed by the TG curve. As shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9</a> (Supporting Information), the whole weight loss from the room temperature to 800 °C is about 29.4&nbsp;wt%. According to the oxidation process of composite material, the content of carbon in NFS-1 is calculated as 15.8&nbsp;wt%, which guaranteed the high electrical conductivity for electron transport.</p>
         
         <p>The structures and morphologies of the as-prepared samples are observed by SEM and TEM. The as-prepared precursor presents the monodisperse microrod morphology with 200–300&nbsp;nm in diameter and 5–10&nbsp;µm in length, while exhibiting the similar diffraction peaks as the reported literature (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10</a>, Supporting Information).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400371-bib-0038" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400371-bib-0038_R_d190217481e918" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 38 - ACS Catal.">38</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> As shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S11a<sub>1</sub></a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S11a<sub>2</sub></a> (Supporting Information), FS exhibits the 1D sharp-cone-like microrod morphology with about 6–10&nbsp;µm in length and 300–500&nbsp;nm in diameter. The surface of FS is consisted of numerous primary nanoparticles and nanosheets, which originated from the metal nodes in the MOF precursor. Moreover, FS delivers a quasi-solid microrod structure as observed from Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S11a<sub>3</sub></a> (Supporting Information). After the Na-doping, NFS-1 also shows the 1D microrod morphology with a rough surface and hollow structure (<b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400371-fig-0003">3a–c</a>), comprising the enlarged primary particles and carbon matrix (derived from the organic ligands). The microrod-like morphology is partially destroyed with the increasing of Na-doping amount. As depicted in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S11b<sub>1</sub>–b<sub>3</sub></a> (Supporting Information), the hollow structure of the rod skeleton in NFS-2 is more obvious under the erosion of double Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub>. N<sub>2</sub> adsorption-desorption analyses of as-prepared samples confirm a decreased Brunauer-Emmett-Teller (BET) surface area of NFS-1 and NFS-2 with regular mesopore distribution, which is consistent with the SEM observation (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S12</a>, Supporting Information). The clear lattice fringes with a spacing of 0.21&nbsp;nm depicted in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S11a<sub>4</sub></a>,<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">b<sub>4</sub></a> (Supporting Information) and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">3d</a> are assigned to the (206) plane of Fe<sub>7</sub>S<sub>8</sub>. HAADF-STEM is carried out to further investigate the structural defects of NFS-1. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400371-fig-0003">3e</a>, NFS-1 displays a clear distribution of Fe and S atoms from [100] direction. The comparison of line profiles in region I and II shows the missing of Fe atom arrangement, confirming the existence of Fe vacancies in NFS-1. Additionally, the EDX elemental mapping demonstrates the uniform distribution of Fe, Na, S, and C in NFS-1 (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400371-fig-0003">3g</a>).</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="aenm202400371-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/deb34a15-537e-418c-8820-8997d96abf3d/aenm202400371-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/deb34a15-537e-418c-8820-8997d96abf3d/aenm202400371-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/181518b3-f734-4989-8e96-5441909b1e3b/aenm202400371-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/deb34a15-537e-418c-8820-8997d96abf3d/aenm202400371-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=aenm202400371-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Faenm.202400371" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a,b) SEM, c) TEM, d) HRTEM and e) HAADF-STEM images of NFS-1, brown and yellow spheres represent Fe and S atoms respectively; f) the corresponding intensity profiles in the marked regions; g) EDX elemental mapping images of NFS-1.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>The electrochemical performances of as-prepared samples are evaluated by CR2032-type coin cells. The initial three CV curves of the as-prepared electrodes at 0.1&nbsp;mV s<sup>−1</sup> in the voltage range of 0.005-2.5&nbsp;V deliver the typical sodiation/desodiation behavior similar to that of iron sulfides based anode materials (<b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400371-fig-0004">4a</a> and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S13</a>, Supporting Information). The first cathodic peak located at 0.88&nbsp;V represents the formation of the solid electrolyte interface (SEI) film and the intercalation of Na<sup>+</sup> into NFS-1. Comparison with the bare FS, the cathodic peak undergoes right shift from 0.86&nbsp;V (FS) to 0.88 (NFS-1) and 0.90&nbsp;V (NFS-2) by the increasing of sodium addition, which indicated the favorable sodiation kinetics through the sodium pre-doped in Fe<sub>7</sub>S<sub>8</sub>. The reduction peak at 0.31&nbsp;V indicates the transformation of Fe<sup>2+</sup>/Fe<sup>3+</sup> into Fe<sup>0</sup> and formation of Fe and Na<sub>2</sub>S.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400371-bib-0039" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400371-bib-0039_R_d190217481e1029" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 39 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">39</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Moreover, the apparent increased intensity of peak current proves an evidence for the accelerated reaction kinetics by the Na-doping. During the following anodic process, the oxidation peaks at 1.38 and 2.11&nbsp;V are assigned to the desodiation processes leading to the formation of Na<sub>2-</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>FeS, and Fe<sub>7</sub>S<sub>8</sub>, respectively.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400371-bib-0040" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400371-bib-0040_R_d190217481e1043" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 40 - Adv. Mater.">40</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The peaks observed at 0.05&nbsp;V (cathodic scan) and 0.076&nbsp;V (anodic scan) are attributed to the co-intercalation/deintercalation of solvated Na<sup>+</sup> ions within the carbon matrix in the ether-based electrolyte, respectively.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400371-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400371-bib-0025_R_d190217481e1050" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - Molecules">25</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Furthermore, the CV curves of NFS-1 show overlapped profiles after first cathodic scanning, indicating the excellent reversibility and stability of the material during the sodiation/desodiation process.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="aenm202400371-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/5ab767d7-b093-414b-86ef-2106fee66a9c/aenm202400371-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/5ab767d7-b093-414b-86ef-2106fee66a9c/aenm202400371-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/c80d5f52-650d-4a2e-9e5b-ee3f18e5a192/aenm202400371-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/5ab767d7-b093-414b-86ef-2106fee66a9c/aenm202400371-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=aenm202400371-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Faenm.202400371" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) CV curves at 0.1&nbsp;mV s<sup>−1</sup> of NFS-1; b) initial charge–discharge curves at 0.05 A g<sup>−1</sup> of FS, NFS-1 and NFS-2; c) cyclic performances at 0.5 A g<sup>−1</sup>; d) rate performances; e) long-term cyclic evaluation of NFS-1 at 10 A g<sup>−1</sup>; f) comparison of electrochemical performances between NFS-1 and reported iron sulfides-based anodes; g) CV curves of NFS-1 at different scanning rates; h) capacitive contribution of NFS-1 at 1&nbsp;mV s<sup>−1</sup>; i) comparison of pseudocapacitive contribution at different scanning rates; j) GITT curves and k) Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficient at different states of FS, NFS-1 and NFS-2.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>NFS-1 delivers the first discharge and charge capacity of 762 and 659 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively, with an initial coulombic efficiency (ICE) of 86.46% (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400371-fig-0004">4b</a>). In comparison, NFS-2 synthesized with the double Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub> exhibits a diminished capacity (738/604 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>) and ICE (81.89%), indicating the negative effect by the excessive amount of Na-doping. However, both NFS-1 and NFS-2 possess the higher ICE values compared to the FS electrode (77.70%, Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S14</a>, Supporting Information). Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400371-fig-0004">4c</a> shows the cyclic evaluation with coulombic efficiency of the as-prepared samples at a current density of 0.5 A g<sup>−1</sup>. Notably, the NFS-1 electrode retains a discharge specific capacity of 674 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> after 200 cycles, which is significantly superior to that of NFS-2 (566 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>) and FS (394 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>) electrodes. The rate performance of NFS-1 is also superior to the counterparts, delivering a specific capacity of 684, 670, 646, 580 and 528 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.5, 1, 2, 5 and 10 A g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400371-fig-0004">4d</a>). When the current density is restored to 0.05 A g<sup>−1</sup>, the capacity of NFS-1 is still rehabilitated. Encouraged by the superior rate performances of NFS-1, a long-term evaluation at 10.0 A g<sup>−1</sup> is conducted (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400371-fig-0004">4e</a>). Impressively, NFS-1 electrode maintains a remarkable capacity of 503 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> after 1500 cycles with 150–155 s per charging/discharging. In comparison with the reported Fe-based anode materials, the competitive and superior electrochemical performances of NFS-1 demonstrate its significant potential for high-power energy storage application (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400371-fig-0004">4f</a>).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400371-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400371-bib-0017_R_d190217481e1138" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Adv. Energy Mater.">17</a>, <a href="#aenm202400371-bib-0041" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400371-bib-0041_R_d190217481e1138" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 41 - Chem. Eng. J.">41</a>-<a href="#aenm202400371-bib-0050" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400371-bib-0050_R_d190217481e1138" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 50 - Nanoscale">50</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <div class="paragraph-element">To understand the superior electrochemical performance of the fabricated samples, CV, GITT and EIS measurements are conducted. The CV curves of NFS-1 at different scanning rate show the similar profiles with a gradually expand tendency (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400371-fig-0004">4g</a>). The anodic peaks around 2.0&nbsp;V of NFS-1 and NFS-2 are merged at the high scanning rate, indicating that the Na-doping enhances the reaction kinetics compared to FS (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S15a,b</a>, Supporting Information). Based on the Equation&nbsp;(<a class="equationLink scrollableLink" href="#aenm202400371-disp-0001" title="Link to equation">1</a>), the contributions of capacitance (the area of <i>k</i><sub>1</sub><i>v</i>) and diffusion (the area of <i>k</i><sub>2</sub><i>v</i><sup>0.5</sup>) would be calculated.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400371-bib-0051" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400371-bib-0051_R_d190217481e1164" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 51 - Rare Met.">51</a>, <a href="#aenm202400371-bib-0052" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400371-bib-0052_R_d190217481e1164" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 52 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">52</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup><div class="inline-equation" id="aenm202400371-disp-0001"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><!--FIGURE-->
                  <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/d70bb641-7b4b-4f43-8eff-8cbaa7805bc9/aenm202400371-math-0001.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:16146832:media:aenm202400371:aenm202400371-math-0001" display="block" location="graphic/aenm202400371-math-0001.png">
                     <semantics>

         </div>
         
         <div class="paragraph-element">As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400371-fig-0004">4h</a>, the pseudo-capacitive contribution ratio is depicted as the red region and quantified as 89.2% to whole charge storage, which is higher than that of FS (82.9%, Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S15c</a>, Supporting Information) and NFS-2 (83.3%, Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S15d</a>, Supporting Information). Furthermore, the capacitive contribution ratio of NFS-1 exhibits a gradual increase from 82.09% to 89.23% by the increasing scanning rate (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400371-fig-0004">4i</a>). The elevated pseudo-capacitive contribution ratios in NFS-1 can be attributed to the presence of Fe vacancies, regular pore distribution and lower Na<sup>+</sup> migration barrier, which provided more actives sites to store charge rapidly. Additionally, all the Nyquist plots of as-prepared electrodes after 100 cycles at 0.5 A g<sup>−1</sup> presents a distinct gap along the X-axis in the high-frequency region, a semicircle in the medium-frequency region and a line in low-frequency region, representing the solution resistance (<i>R</i><sub>s</sub>), charge transfer resistance (<i>R</i><sub>ct</sub>) and Warburg impedance,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400371-bib-0053" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400371-bib-0053_R_d190217481e1217" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 53 - Nanoscale">53</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> respectively (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S16</a>, Supporting Information). Notably, the smallest diameter of semicircle of NFS-1 indicates its lowest <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> after the cycles to facilitate the electrons/ions transport. In Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400371-fig-0004">4j</a>, the GITT curves of all electrodes exhibit the similar profile as their corresponding charge-discharge curves. Based on Equation&nbsp;(<a class="equationLink scrollableLink" href="#aenm202400371-disp-0002" title="Link to equation">2</a>), the diffusion coefficients of sodium ion (<i>D</i><sub>Na</sub><sup>+</sup>) at different charge/discharge states are calculated and depicted in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400371-fig-0004">4k</a>. All the samples present the same trend as the variations of charge/discharge states.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400371-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400371-bib-0020_R_d190217481e1243" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Adv. Sci.">20</a>, <a href="#aenm202400371-bib-0054" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400371-bib-0054_R_d190217481e1243" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 54 - Chem. Eng. J.">54</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Clearly, NFS-1 processes the highest value of <i>D</i><sub>Na</sub><sup>+</sup>, indicating that the rational Na-doping promotes the transfer rate of Na<sup>+</sup> in the internal phase by the modulation of material band energy structure.

            <div class="inline-equation" id="aenm202400371-disp-0002"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><!--FIGURE-->

                     </semantics></math></span><span class="inline-equation__label">(2)</span></div>
         </div>
         
         <p>Furthermore, the universality of alkali metal doping on weakening Fe─S bond strength is also investigated. After the addition of Li<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub> and K<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub>, the Li-doped Fe<sub>7</sub>S<sub>8</sub> (denoted as LFS) and K-doped Fe<sub>7</sub>S<sub>8</sub> (denoted as KFS) exhibit the same XRD diffraction profile as the bare Fe<sub>7</sub>S<sub>8</sub> without any impurity (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S17</a>, Supporting Information). Notably, the most prominent diffraction peak for both LFS and KFS also shifts to the left, indicating the enlarged lattice patterns affected by the Li/K doping. All the alkali metal doped samples exhibit enhanced capacity and cyclic stability compared to the bare Fe<sub>7</sub>S<sub>8</sub> (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S18</a>, Supporting Information), which would be attributed to the similar effect of weakening M─S bonds. The NFS-1 electrode delivers a capacity of 593 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 50th cycle, significantly higher than that of the LFS (498 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>) and KFS (541 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>), demonstrating the superior suitability of the sodium doping for Fe<sub>7</sub>S<sub>8</sub> in the field of SIBs anode.</p>
         
         <p>The sodium storage performances of NFS-1 in full cells are further conducted pairing with the commercial Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> (<b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400371-fig-0005">5a</a>), which exhibited pure NASICON structure,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400371-bib-0055" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400371-bib-0055_R_d190217481e1375" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 55 - Adv. Energy Mater.">55</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> spherical porous morphology and stable electrochemical properties (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S19</a>, Supporting Information). Both current density and capacity are calculated based on the mass of the anode material. The typical charge-discharge curves of cathode, anode and full cell are depicted in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400371-fig-0005">5b</a>. Impressively, the Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>║ NFS-1 full cell delivers the high charge/discharge capacity of 524/498 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> with a 95% ICE in the voltage range of 0.3-3.6&nbsp;V at 0.1 A g<sup>−1</sup> (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S20</a>, Supporting Information). As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400371-fig-0005">5c</a>, the full cell exhibits an outstanding cycling performance, with a capacity of 451 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> after 100 cycles at 0.5 A g<sup>−1</sup>, and remarkable capacity retention (approximately 95.9%). Furthermore, the full-cell shows the reversible capacities of 498, 491, 461, 424, 379, and 283&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup> at current densities of 0.1, 0.2, 0.5, 1, 2, and 5 A g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively. Even after rate tests and 965 cycles at 5 A g<sup>−1</sup>, the cell still maintains a reversible capacity of 148&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup>, with a capacity fading of only 0.043% per cycle (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400371-fig-0005">5d</a>). The excellent rate performance with the stable voltage plateau (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400371-fig-0005">5e</a>) and long-term cycle stability of Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>║ NFS-1 full cell suggests the significant potential practical applications of Na-doped Fe<sub>7</sub>S<sub>8</sub> in SIBs.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="aenm202400371-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/e346897d-1a7c-4062-a17f-66634a11615b/aenm202400371-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/e346897d-1a7c-4062-a17f-66634a11615b/aenm202400371-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/bbf824dd-a3d0-495b-ba22-2268d0661451/aenm202400371-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/e346897d-1a7c-4062-a17f-66634a11615b/aenm202400371-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=aenm202400371-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Faenm.202400371" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) Schematic illustration of the Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>3</sub>║NFS-1coin full cell; b) charge–discharge curves, c) cyclic performance, d) rate performance and e) charge–discharge curves at different current densities of the full cells.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>The FS and NFS-1 half cells after 50 cycles at 0.5 A g<sup>−1</sup> are dissected and characterized using SEM to investigate the morphological and structural evolution. Clearly, the cross-section SEM images of cycled electrodes (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S21a,b</a>, Supporting Information) reveal a better capability to tolerance volumetric expansion of NFS-1 (thickness increased by 25.6%) than that of counterpart (FS, 46.4%). In comparison to the broken and pulverized rod-likes structure observed in FS, the well-maintained microrod of NFS-1 after cycles indicates the improved structural stability through Na-doping (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S21c,d</a>, Supporting Information). Moreover, the XPS Fe 2p spectrum after Ar<sup>+</sup> etching of NFS-1 shows a low intensity of Fe-S characterized peak, indicating the persistent influence of Na-doping on the Fe─S bond strength (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S22</a>, Supporting Information). Significantly, the Fe─S bond strength of FS decreases obviously after 50 cycles, coinciding with the ending of capacity fading in cyclic test. This observed phenomenon would present a new perspective on the query of metal sulfides capacity fluctuation during the cycles. The strength of M─S bond in metal sulfides is reducing with the Na<sup>+</sup> repeated insertion processes, thereby facilitating the M─S bond breaking to participate the sodiation reaction in the initial cycles. The pre-doping of sodium in Fe<sub>7</sub>S<sub>8</sub> has partially replaced the process of electrochemical activation, enabling NFS-1 to exhibit a stable performance during the initial cyclic stage. Furthermore, the structural stability of NFS-1 is continued to be observed after extended cycles. As shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S23a,b</a> (Supporting Information), NFS still deliver a rod-like morphology after 100 cycles. NFS-1 primary particles are supported by the carbon matrix to prevent the agglomeration between active substances (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S23c</a>, Supporting Information). Notably, NFS-1 exhibits the distinct lattice firings with the spacing of 0.30 and 0.26&nbsp;nm, assigning to the (200) and (203) plane of Fe<sub>7</sub>S<sub>8</sub>, further demonstrating the structural stability and reversibility of NFS-1 anode (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S23d</a>, Supporting Information).</p>
         
         <p>The influences of Na doping on the formation of SEI films are also investigated by employing XPS with Ar<sup>+</sup> etching for 60 s. The formation of organic components in SEI films would be reflected in combination with the changes of C 1s and O 1s spectra before and after etching. As depicted in <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400371-fig-0006">6a</a>, the intensity of Na<i><sub>x</sub></i>C peak (Na<sup>+</sup> insertion into carbon) after etching in NFS-1 is much higher than that of FS, despite both delivering the similar intensity of Na<i><sub>x</sub></i>C peak before etching. Besides, based on the proportion of peak area, the relative content of C─O─Na (the main component of SEI films) in NFS-1 is decreased from 49.4% to 37.2% after the etching (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400371-fig-0006">6b</a>), which is lower than that of FS (decreasing from 49.6% to 40.8%). Considering the observed variation trend of Na<sub>x</sub>C and C-O-Na peaks, it would be inferred that NFS-1 processes a thinner SEI film than FS.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400371-bib-0056" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400371-bib-0056_R_d190217481e1528" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 56 - Adv. Energy Mater.">56</a>, <a href="#aenm202400371-bib-0057" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400371-bib-0057_R_d190217481e1528" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 57 - Adv. Mater.">57</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The more exposure of Na<sub>x</sub>C and more etched C─O─Na component under the same etching condition are strong support for this conjecture. Moreover, NaF is widely considered as a crucial component to strengthen the SEI films and facilitate the Na<sup>+</sup> transfer.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400371-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400371-bib-0005_R_d190217481e1538" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">5</a>, <a href="#aenm202400371-bib-0058" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400371-bib-0058_R_d190217481e1538" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 58 - Adv. Mater.">58</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In the F 1s and P 2p spectra (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400371-fig-0005">5c,d</a>), the presence of Na<i><sub>x</sub></i>PF<i><sub>y</sub></i> and PF<i><sub>x</sub></i>O<i><sub>y</sub></i> component originates from the decomposition of the electrolyte NaPF<sub>6</sub> salt. As shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S24</a> (Supporting Information), at the pre-etching state, the content of F and P atom in NFS-1 is about 4.0 at.% and 0.9 at.%, respectively, which is smaller than the bare FS (F: 6.6 at.%, P: 1.2 at.%), demonstrating the reduced decomposition of electrolyte on the NFS-1 surface. After the etching, NFS-1 exhibits the higher relative proportion of NaF and PO<i><sub>x</sub></i> component than that of the counterpart (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400371-fig-0006">6c,d</a> and Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a>, Supporting Information), indicating the formation of the more stable SEI films.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400371-bib-0059" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400371-bib-0059_R_d190217481e1574" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 59 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">59</a>, <a href="#aenm202400371-bib-0060" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400371-bib-0060_R_d190217481e1574" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 60 - Adv. Energy Mater.">60</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> DFT calcuations of NaF adosrption energy on the electrode surface further confirm these analyses. NFS-1 presents a higher adsorption energy to faciliate the formation of an SEI film with rich-inorganic content (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S25</a>, Supporting Information). In summary, the SEI films of NFS-1 are thinner and stable with higher inorganic components to achieve the superior electrochemical performances (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400371-fig-0006">6e</a>). The Na-doping would be regard as the key factor to form the thin and stable SEI films with an increased inorganic content during the initial cycles, which alleviates the electrolyte decomposition and induces the ordered deposition of sodium-containing components through the pre-doped sodium, simultaneously.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="aenm202400371-fig-0006"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/f6f63430-ce37-4a77-9ef0-228855f233a2/aenm202400371-fig-0006-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/f6f63430-ce37-4a77-9ef0-228855f233a2/aenm202400371-fig-0006-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/797ce4e0-dc1a-4750-9301-2f841d4b277d/aenm202400371-fig-0006-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/f6f63430-ce37-4a77-9ef0-228855f233a2/aenm202400371-fig-0006-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 6<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=aenm202400371-fig-0006&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Faenm.202400371" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">XPS spectra of the FS and NFS-1 cycled electrodes, a) C 1s, b) O 1s, c) F 1s and d) P 2p; e) schematic illustration for the SEI films formation.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
      </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="aenm202400371-sec-0030">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="aenm202400371-sec-0030-title">3 Conclusion</h2>
         
         <p>In conclusion, the alkali metal (Li, Na, K) doped Fe<sub>7</sub>S<sub>8</sub> anode materials are constructed through a facile solid phase sulfuration, which exhibit the improved sodium storage performances than the bare sample. The role of Na-doping is thoroughly investigated through experimental analysis and DFT calculation. Rational Na-doping in Fe<sub>7</sub>S<sub>8</sub> anode effectively modulates its energy band structure, induces the formation of iron vacancies and weakens the strength of Fe─S bond to accelerate both sodiation reaction kinetics and Na<sup>+</sup>/electrons transport rate, simultaneously. Moreover, the pre-doping sodium deliver a strong guiding effect on the formation of thin and stable SEI films. Combing with the Fe-MOFs derived porous carbon matrix, the optimized NFS-1 exhibits the excellent electrochemical performance, including an increased ICE (from 77.7% to 86.46%), high-rate capability (528 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 10 A g<sup>−1</sup>) and long cycling stability (503 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> over 1500 cycles at 10 A g<sup>−1</sup>). Furthermore, these superior properties are also reflected in Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>║ NFS-1 cells, with a high reversible capacity of 451 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> after 100 cycles at 0.5 A g<sup>−1</sup> (95.9% capacity retention). This work provides a promising design and doping strategy to achieve the advanced anode materials with superior performance in sodium-ion batteries.</p>
         </section>
      <div class="article-section__content">
